HSM.pdf</p></div></div></div><div class="stElementContainer element-container st-emotion-cache-kj6hex eu6p4el1" data-testid="stElementContainer" data-stale="false" width="auto"><div class="stMarkdown" data-testid="stMarkdown"><div data-testid="stMarkdownContainer" class="st-emotion-cache-1104ytp e1icttdg0"><p>UNIT ONE: INTRODUCTION TO LEADERSHIP AND MANAGEMENT</p>
<p>Purpose:</p>
<p>The purpose of this unit is to equip the learner with knowledge and skills in leadership and management practices and behaviours and activities that are carried out by managers at all levels in an organization.</p>
<p>Expected learning outcomes</p>
<p>By the end of this unit, the learner should be able to:</p>
<ol>
<li>Describe the concepts of leadership and management</li>
<li>Explain the relationship between leadership and management</li>
<li>Explain the theories of leadership</li>
<li>Discuss the styles of leadership</li>
<li>Explain the principles of management</li>
<li>Explain the concepts of change management;</li>
<li>Explain the roles and functions of leaders and managers</li>
<li>Organizational behavior and group dynamics- team formation; Mission and vision.</li>
</ol>
<p>Concepts of leadership and management</p>
<p>Leadership and management are often regarded as interchangeable which is not the case. While the most effective managers are usually also good leaders, this is not necessarily the case. Nor is a good leader always a good manager.</p>
<p>Management</p>
<p>Originally, the term management comes from Latin word "manus" referring to the driver controlling a team of horses pulling a cart. This image helps us in considering other definitions. The examples of definitions of management as defined differently by different scholars are that, it is the;</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Process of getting things done through other people (in simple terms).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Process through which resources in an organization are made available and effectively used in accomplishing the organizational goals.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Process of working with and through individual and groups and other resources to accomplish organizational goals or objectives.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Planning, organizing, co-ordinating, commanding and controlling.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Process of creating and maintaining of an internal environment of an organization in which individuals and groups perform effectively towards the attainment of the organization goals.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Process of integrating (bring together) all the organizational aspects for instance the structure, people and technology in order to achieve the organizational goals.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>It should be noted that, the definitions of management are non-exhaustive, they vary greatly because of the nature, and types of organizations in terms of products produced or services rendered. However, the most important elements in most definitions are: -</p>
<ul>
<li>Process</li>
<li>Resources</li>
<li>Utilization and</li>
<li>Goals</li>
</ul>
<p>Management is important because it enhances the efficiency and effectiveness of an organization's performance. It involves planning, leading, organizing and controlling an organization, as well as more the intangible qualities of inspiration and motivation. Regarding management as a process helps to ensure that operations achieve their objectives and that essential health systems are improved – the operational aspect of management.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="who-is-a-manager-and-what-are-their-roles">Who Is a Manager and What Are Their Roles?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#who-is-a-manager-and-what-are-their-roles" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A manager is a person who has the responsibility for achieving certain outcomes, along with the authority to utilize the resources of the organization in doing so. The combination of responsibility and authority is crucial to the concept of management. Timeliness is another critical aspect. The timely use of the resources – whether human, financial, information or physical assets – is essential for effective management. In an ideal management team, the team members recognize the authority of the manager and provide support in a constructive way.</p>
<p>A manager is therefore a person who can organize people to work together harmoniously and make effective use of resources to achieve laid-down objectives, through a process that includes planning, implementation, monitoring and evaluation. A distinction should be made between a manager who is a leader, and one who is an administrator. Administration is a subset of management. An administrator is somebody who interprets policies and directives from above for implementation, knows the rules and applies them well. The administrator and the manager do not have to be different people.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="process-management">Process Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#process-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Process management means documenting processes (the way work should be done in the organization), measuring the results of that work as seen by the end users, target populations and policy makers, and taking corrective action if results don't meet expectations. In a well-managed organization, all staff members have two responsibilities – to do their job and to improve their work processes. It is upon the management to direct and support staff in realizing these responsibilities.</p>
<p>A health system is managed by a team and will be most productive and efficient if the team focuses on technique – the way the team members work together, the methods through which services are delivered. It is very easy to focus on what you do. It is more important to focus on why you do it. To-day the paradigm of health care is becoming more client-centred, a concept that leads to demands for client-oriented, health promoting and higher quality health care services. Thus managers need to keep focused on the population for whom they are working to reduce suffering and mortality caused by disease. Continuous improvement is at the heart of process management.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="theories-of-management">THEORIES OF MANAGEMENT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#theories-of-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There are various management theories as listed below:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Classical management.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Human Relations</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Situational/Contingency.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Management by Objectives</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Systems approach to management</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Bureaucratic Management</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-classical-approach">1. Classical approach<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-classical-approach" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This approach was developed between 1900 and 1910 and extended through to early 1950s. The theory had 3 branches. These are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Scientific Management</li>
<li>Administrative/Principles of Management</li>
<li>Bureaucratic Management</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="a-scientific-management-theory">a. Scientific management theory<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-scientific-management-theory" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>This is a technique of management whereby the work methods are scientifically studied and standards established. It is a philosophy about the relationship between the people and work - not technique or efficiency device.</p>
<p>The scientific Management approach was developed by an American engineer called Frederick Winslow Taylor, (1856 - 1915). He came up with this theory because the systematic approach (formally used) failed to lead to widespread production efficiency. He discovered that:</p>
<ul>
<li>Production and pay were poor.</li>
<li>Inefficiency and waste were common.</li>
<li>Many companies had unused potential.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="principles-of-scientific-management-theory">Principles of Scientific management theory<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#principles-of-scientific-management-theory" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The scientific management theory has 4 principles. These are: -</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Developing scientific methods of designing jobs to replace the rule of thumb methods. This involves collecting and analyzing data to arrive at the best way of performing tasks or series of tasks.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The Scientific selection and progressive teaching and developing employees to improve performance. Integrating scientifically selected employees and developed methods of designing jobs. The Scientific methods of job design should be well explained by management to reduce resistance.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>A division of work resulting in the interdependence between management and workers. Taylor believed that if they were truly dependent on one another, then co-operation would naturally follow.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>NOTE:</p>
<ol>
<li>Scientific management theory emphasized on specialization and division of labour.</li>
<li>Taylor and other scientific management pioneers believed that employees could be motivated by economic rewards, provided those rewards were related to individual performance. "The more you work the more you earn".</li>
</ol>
<p>Limitations of scientific management</p>
<p>These are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Simplistic motivational assumptions.</li>
<li>Workers were viewed as part of machine.</li>
<li>Exploitation of labour.</li>
<li>Ignored relationship between the organizations and its environment.</li>
<li>Excluded senior management tasks.</li>
</ul>
<p>These limitations prompted the development of administrative management theory.</p>
<p>b. Administrative/principles of management theory</p>
<p>It is also referred to as principles of management. This type of management places emphasis on the members of the organization usually referred to as "body corporate", that is, the personnel or employees. The Administrative theory was developed by a French top Management specialist called Henri Fayol, (1841-1925). The theory emerged in 1916 and it emphasized the perspective of senior managers within the organization. Taylor argued that management was a profession and could be taught.</p>
<p>Fayol in his contribution, defined management as comprising of five (5) elements. These are:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Planning</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Organizing</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Commanding</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Coordinating and</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Controlling</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>In addition, Fayol stated 14 principles that he wanted them applied flexibly. These are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Division of work - Divide work into specialized tasks (elements) and assign responsibility to specific individual.</li>
<li>Authority - Delegate authority a long with responsibility (workers are given the right to command to get the work done).</li>
<li>Discipline - Rules and regulations should be enforced fairly and judiciously. There should be well-defined procedures of dealing with disciplinary matters.</li>
<li>Unity of command - Each employee should be assigned to only one supervisor, otherwise conflict and confusion in authority and instructions would result.</li>
<li>Unity of direction - The principle of Fayol states that “there should be one head and one plan for group of activities/tasks having the same objectives. For example, the personnel department should have only one personnel officer with a specified plan for personnel policies, feeding personnel in all departments of the organization.</li>
<li>Subordination of individual interest to organization interest - Interests of the organization must take priority over the interests of an individual or a group, whenever there is a conflict between the two.</li>
<li>Remuneration of employees - All workers should be fairly paid with appropriate additional incentives for additional efforts.</li>
<li>Centralization - All major policy formulation and decisions should be made at the top management level (central point).</li>
<li>Scalar chain - Communication should follow the formal channel in the hierarchy. Keep the communication within the chain of command.</li>
<li>Order - Place for everything and everything in its place.</li>
<li>Equity - Fair discipline and order enhance employee commitment. Managers should be fair and friendly to subordinates and they should exercise impartiality.</li>
<li>Initiative - Encourage employees to act on their own in support of the organization's direction regardless of the mistakes made.</li>
<li>Esprit de corps</li>
</ul>
<p>Promote a unity of interests between employees and management. Workers should work as a team.</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Stability of staff/tenure of employees</strong>
Employee turnover should be minimized. Tenure and long term commitment should be encouraged. It results in a sense of belonging to organization giving rise to dedication and better outcome/results/output.</li>
</ul>
<p>Although in his contributions, management was viewed as a profession that could be trained and developed, emphasized the broad policy aspect of top - level managers and offered universal management prescriptions, it had limitation: -</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p><strong>Limitation</strong>
The universal prescriptions need qualifications for environmental, technological and personnel factors.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>c. Bureaucratic management theory</strong>
It was developed by a German Sociologist, Lawyer and Historian called Max Weber (1864 - 1924). He was interested in the work of Taylor. His theory was based on authority structures (hierarchy) of a form of an organization. He was specifically concerned with the administration, that is, how the organizations are structured in terms of “who is responsible for who or to who along with the activities to be carried out.” He thought bureaucracy would be:</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>i) Fairer</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>ii) More impartial and</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>iii) More rational</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Characteristics of bureaucratic theory</strong></p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Workers are selected and promoted on the basis of technical competence and experience (merit).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>A system of rules covering the duties and rights of employees that must be adhered to.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Well defined hierarchy of authority.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>A division of labour by functional specialization.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>A system of procedures for dealing with work situations.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Job-holders are expected to be impartial, that is, practice fairness and no favourism.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="limitations">Limitations<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#limitations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The shortcomings to this theory are:</p>
<ul>
<li>It had limited organizational flexibility and it is slow in decision making.</li>
<li>It ignores the importance of people and interpersonal relations.</li>
<li>It accumulates power that could result in authoritarian management.</li>
<li>Rules may become ends in themselves.</li>
<li>Difficult to dismantle once established.</li>
<li>Too much specialization that could result, for example, patient to die in hospital if the person assigned to that responsibility is not there.</li>
</ul>
<p>These limitations come because of the complexity of organizations. It is better for small organizations, although it is mainly applied in the government organizations.</p>
<p>Bureaucratic theory served to fit the traditional organizations which were less innovative in their operations and later around 1930's, human relations theory was developed to look at the human aspect of the organization.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-human-relation-theory">2. Human relation theory<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-human-relation-theory" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The theory was developed by Professor Elton Mayo around 1930s and it was backed up by A. Maslow, Mcgregor (1960), Hersberg, Likert, Argyris and Chester Bernard (1938).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management-functions">Management Functions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-functions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Managerial work consists of a number of well-defined but interrelated functions (see Figure 1 below):</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Planning</strong>: Working in a broad outline of the things that need to be done and the methods for doing them to accomplish the purpose set for organization.</li>
<li><strong>Organizing</strong>: At the senior most level, establishing the formal structure of an organization through which work subdivisions are arranged, defined and coordinated for the whole</li>
</ul>
<p>organization; at lower levels carrying out similar functions for the specific department or division.</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Guiding</strong>: On an ongoing basis, making decisions, embodying them in specific general orders and instructions, and serving as a leader of the organization.</li>
<li><strong>Controlling</strong>: Coordinating, monitoring, evaluating, and supervising.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-management-by-objectives">3. Management by Objectives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-management-by-objectives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In this management approach the manager sets clear objectives to be realized. This is repeated in every department in the organization to help realize the broad organizational goal. It is worth noting that objectives must be SMART so as to ensure the organization realizes its objectives. To recap, SMART refers to:</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Specific</strong>: The objective is exact, distinct and clearly stated. Vague language or generalities are not results. It identifies the nature of expected changes, the target, region, etc. It should be as detailed as possible without being wordy.</li>
<li><strong>Measurable</strong>: The objective is measurable qualitatively and/or quantitatively.</li>
<li><strong>Achievable</strong>: The objective is achievable with the human and financial resources available.</li>
<li><strong>Realistic</strong>: The objective addresses specific, recognized challenges and is within mandate.</li>
<li><strong>Time-bound</strong>: The objective is to be achieved in a stated time-frame or planning period</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>NB</strong>—Management by objectives is a goal oriented process and not works oriented process. To be busy and work is not important if it does not effectively lead to achievement. It is an aid to planning as well as a motivating factor for employees</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="elements-of-mbo-process">ELEMENTS OF MBO PROCESS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#elements-of-mbo-process" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Central goal setting</strong>—this is the basic stage in MBO process where central management sets the definition and clarification of the organizational objectives usually in consultation with other managers (group and top manager jointly involved). The objectives should be specific and realistic...idea should be floated from top to bottom</p>
<p><strong>Manager-subordinate involvement</strong>—here the subordinates work with the managers in setting</p>
<p>individual goals. These goals are specific and short range and primarily indicate what the subordinates must set goals in consultation with individuals who comprise his unit. In this manner everyone is involved in the goal setting</p>
<p><strong>Matching goals and resources</strong>—objectives can only be achieved if resources are available. There4 management should provide the subordinates with the necessary tools and materials to effectively achieve these goals. The resources should be allocated precisely in line with the goals set and should be done in consultation with the subordinates</p>
<p><strong>Freedom of implementation</strong>—there should be adequate freedom for the manager subordinate task force in deciding on the utilization of resources and means of achieving the objectives</p>
<p><strong>Review and appraisal of performance</strong>—there should be regular reviews of progress between managers and subordinates. These reviews would:</p>
<ul>
<li>Determine if the individual is making satisfactory progress</li>
<li>Reveals if any unanticipated problems have developed</li>
<li>Help subordinates understand the process of MBO better</li>
<li>Improves morale of the subordinates</li>
<li>Assists the managers and subordinates to modify either objectives or methods if need be</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="principles-of-management">Principles of Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#principles-of-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Basic principles of management were set out by Henry Fayol (1841–1925), a French management theorist who developed one of the first comprehensive statements of a general theory of management.¹ Still relevant today, the 14 principles Fayol articulated are:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p><strong>Division of work</strong>: The specialization of the workforce according to the skills a person has, creating specific personal and professional development within the labour force and therefore increasing productivity; leads to specialization, which increases the efficiency of labour. By separating a small part of work, the workers' speed and accuracy in its performance increases. This principle is applicable to both technical and managerial work.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Authority and responsibility</strong>: The issue of commands followed by responsibility for their consequences. Authority means the right of a superior to give orders to subordinates; responsibility means obligation for performance. This principle suggests that there must be</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>parity between authority and responsibility. They go together and are two sides of the same coin.</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p><strong>Discipline</strong>: Discipline refers to obedience, proper conduct in relation to others, respect of authority, etc. It is essential for the smooth functioning of all organizations.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Unity of command</strong>: This principle states that every subordinate should receive orders and be accountable to one and only one superior. If an employee receives orders from more than one superior, it is likely to create confusion and conflict. Unity of command also makes it easier to fix responsibility for mistakes.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Unity of direction</strong>: All those working in the same line of activity must understand and pursue the same objectives. All related activities should be put under one group, there should be one plan of action for them, and they should be under the control of one manager. Unity of action seeks to ensure a focus of efforts and coordination of strength.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Subordination of individual interest</strong>: The management must put aside personal considerations and put company objectives first. Therefore the interests of the goals of the organization must prevail over the personal interests of individuals.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Remuneration</strong>: Workers must be paid sufficiently as this is one of the chief motivations of employment and therefore greatly influences productivity. The quantum and methods of remuneration payable should be fair, reasonable and rewarding of effort.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>The degree of centralization</strong>: The amount of power wielded within the central management depends on company size. Centralization implies the concentration of decision making authority at the top management. Sharing of authority with lower levels is called decentralization. The organization should strive to achieve a proper balance.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Scalar chain</strong>: Scalar chain refers to the chain of superiors ranging from top management to the lowest rank. The principle suggests that there should be a clear line of authority from top to bottom linking all managers at all levels. It is considered a chain of command. It involves aconcept called a "gang plank", through which a subordinate may contact a superior or the superior's superior in case of an emergency, defying the hierarchy of control. However, the immediate superiors must be informed about the matter.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Order</strong>: Social order ensures the fluid operation of a company through authoritative procedure. Material order ensures safety and efficiency in the workplace.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Equity: Employees must be treated kindly, and justice must be enacted to ensure a just work-place. Managers should be fair and impartial when dealing with employees.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Stability of tenure of personnel: The period of service should not be too short and employees should not be moved from positions frequently. An employee who is removed before becoming accustomed to the assigned work cannot render useful service.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Initiative: Tapping into the initiative of employees can add strength and new ideas to an organization. Initiative on the part of employees is a source of strength for the organization because it provides new and better ideas. Employees who know their ideas are respected are likely to take greater interest in the functioning of the organization.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Esprit de corps: This refers to the need of managers to develop and ensure morale in the work-place, individually and communally. Team spirit helps develop an atmosphere of mutual trust and understanding. These can be used to initiate and aid the processes of change, organization, decision making, skill management and the overall view of the management function.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="managerial-roles">Managerial Roles<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#managerial-roles" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Managers clearly have many functions and roles. These can be summarized into three basic categories:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="interpersonal-roles">Interpersonal roles:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#interpersonal-roles" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Managers as leaders, providing guidance and motivation.</li>
<li>Managers as liaison officers, maintaining a web of relationships with individuals and groups.</li>
<li>Managers as disturbance handlers, deal with situations and change beyond their control</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="informational-roles">Informational roles:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#informational-roles" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Managers as monitors, continually seeking and receiving information as a basis for action.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Managers as disseminators, passing factual information to supervisors, colleagues and subordinates and transmitting value statements to guide subordinates in making decisions.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Managers as spokespeople, transmitting information into their organization's environment.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="decision-roles">Decision roles:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#decision-roles" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Managers as entrepreneurs, acting as initiators of controlled change in the organization.</li>
<li>Managers as resource allocators, making choices about scheduling their own time, authorizing actions and allocating people and finance to projects or activities.</li>
<li>Managers as negotiators with other organizations or individuals.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management-skills">Management Skills<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-skills" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>For managers to be effective they require certain critical skills, including those in the list below. The skills may be used singly or in combination, depending on the situation and circumstances. They are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Computer skills</li>
<li>Technical skills</li>
<li>Human skills</li>
<li>Communication skills</li>
<li>Interpersonal skills</li>
</ul>
<p>Management can therefore be classified into three skills namely</p>
<ol>
<li>Technical skills - the ability and knowledge in using equipment, techniques and procedures involved in performing specific tasks. Focuses on job.</li>
<li>Human skills - the ability to work effectively with other people as individuals or group. Its main focus is people. They include¹
a. Interpersonal skills – ability to communicate with, understand, and motivate both individuals and groups.
b. Emotional intelligence – comprises of attributes such as self-awareness, self-regulation, motivation, empathy, and social skills
i. Self- awareness – the ability to monitor, notice, and label one's feelings as they occur. This allows one to be more certain about feelings and to identify early vague feelings</li>
</ol>
<p>¹ Sison P. S. (1991). Personnel and Human Resource Management</p>
<p>ii. Self-regulation – the ability to manage one's emotions and impulses. A person with this skill is often viewed as being reflective, comfortable with change and ambiguity, and able to control impulsiveness</p>
<p>iii. Motivation – being highly motivated is essential for focusing attention, mastering situations, showing creativity, and being productive and successful</p>
<p>iv. Empathy – the ability to recognize emotions in others. This is important for teamwork as well as helping adjust one's behavior to the emerging reactions of others</p>
<p>v. Social skills – the ability to handle relationships with others is central to being perceived as popular, effective with others, and having the qualities of a leader.²</p>
<p>c. Communication skills – manager's ability both to effectively convey ideas and information to others and to effectively receive ideas and information from others</p>
<ol start="3">
<li>Conceptual skills – ability to see the whole organization and the inter-relationships between its parts. It focuses on ideas³. It is also the ability to think in abstract. They include</li>
</ol>
<p>a. Diagnostic skills – manager's ability to visualize the most appropriate response to a situation</p>
<p>b. Decision-making skills – manager's ability to correctly recognize and define problems and opportunities and to then select an appropriate course of action to solve problems and capitalize on opportunities⁴</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="leadership">Leadership<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#leadership" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="definition">Definition:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#definition" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Leading is the process of influencing people, that is, personnel, followers so that they could</p>
<p>²Odgers P. (2005). Administrative office Management: Complete Course 13th Edition</p>
<p>³Saleemi N.A. (2010). Principles and Practice of Management Simplified</p>
<p>⁴Odgers P. (2005). Administrative office Management: Complete Course 13th Edition</p>
<p>contribute to the goals of the organization or group. Leadership on the other hand is the interpersonal influence, exercised in situations and directed through the communication process towards the organizational goals. It is concerned with guiding others or activities of followers/subordinates towards the pre-determined objectives/goals. A leader/manager must use influence (positive) to achieve the set objectives or goals.</p>
<p>However, there are as many definitions of leadership as there are people who have attempted to define it. One common theme that runs through each of them is the ability of an individual to inspire confidence and to provide support to members of a group to achieve the programme objectives.</p>
<p>Leadership is a process or ability to influence the behaviour of others, to motivate and mobilize others to work together and achieve a common aim. It is a way of focusing and motivating a group of people to enable them to achieve their own aims and develop themselves. It also involves being accountable and responsible for the group as a whole. Leadership comprises three factors: the leader, the people, and the situation or conditions. Thus leadership is determined not only by the characteristics of the leader and the team, but also by the situations that prevail in the programme or organization.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="process-of-leading">Process of leading<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#process-of-leading" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Leading involves various activities and not merely leading people. It comprises of:</p>
<ul>
<li>Planning</li>
<li>Organizing</li>
<li>Directing</li>
<li>Delegating</li>
<li>Reporting</li>
<li>Staffing and</li>
<li>Motivating people</li>
</ul>
<p>Great vision emerges when a powerful mind, working long and hard on massive amount of</p>
<p>information, is able to see (or recognize in suggestions from others) interesting patterns and new possibilities <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mrow></mrow><mn>5</mn></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">^5</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8141em;"></span><span class="mord"><span></span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.8141em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight">5</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span>.</p>
<p>A leader uses power in influencing group behavior. Power the ability to influence, command, or apply force is central to leadership and derives from several resources, including: <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mrow></mrow><mn>6</mn></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">^6</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8141em;"></span><span class="mord"><span></span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.8141em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight">6</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></p>
<ol>
<li>Authority or legitimate power – comes from the right of the position in the organization to direct the activities of subordinates</li>
<li>Reward power – based on leader's ability to withhold or provide rewards for performance</li>
<li>Coercive power – maintains control over punishment</li>
<li>Referent power – exists when the leader possesses personal characteristics that are appealing to the constituency, and the constituency flows out of admiration, charisma impact, or the desire to be like the leader.</li>
<li>Expert power – occurs when the leader has knowledge or expertise that is of value</li>
<li>Information power – based on the persuasive content of the person's message, apart from personal characteristics.</li>
</ol>
<p>Power can also be referred to a measure of a person's potential to get others to do what he or she wants them to do, as well as to avoid being forced by others to do what he or she does not want to do.</p>
<p>Effective leadership in organizations creates a vision of the future that considers the legitimate long-term interests of the parties involved in the organization, develops a strategy for moving toward that vision, enlists the support of employees to produce the movement, and motivates the employees to implement the strategy.</p>
<p>In summary leadership can be defined as a process by which a person influences others to accomplish an objective and directs the organization in a way that makes it more cohesive and coherent. This definition is similar to Northouse's (2007, p3) definition — Leadership is a process whereby an individual influences a group of individuals to achieve a common goal.</p>
<p><span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mrow></mrow><mn>5</mn></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">^5</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8141em;"></span><span class="mord"><span></span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.8141em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight">5</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span> Gill R. (2006). Theory and Practice of Leadership
<span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mrow></mrow><mn>6</mn></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">^6</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8141em;"></span><span class="mord"><span></span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.8141em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight">6</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span> Vershinina L., Kendrick M., &amp; Daft L. R. (2010). Management</p>
<p>Are Leaders Born or Made?</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Some argue that leaders are born, while others hold that leaders are made. Whichever the case, the training of leaders – whether born or not – gives them skills that help them to become better leaders or managers.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>To maximize their potential individuals must begin to think of leadership in place of management, accountability in place of responsibility, job roles in place of job descriptions.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>To increase our leadership ability, we must expand ourselves. Leadership development is personal development. In order for us to understand ourselves, we must gather information; we must obtain the facts and data that tell us about ourselves. For individuals to maximize potential they must begin to think of leadership in place of management, of accountability in place of responsibility. They must begin to think of job roles in place of job descriptions</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Leadership is the art of inspiring followers to perform their duties willingly, competently and enthusiastically so as to achieve organizational objectives. Leadership is determined not only by the characteristics of the leader and the team, but also by the situations that prevail. A good leader has the ability to set direction and inspire others. The leader is willing to take risk, is creative and innovative, accountable and responsible. In a service-oriented organization, leadership determines the success or failure of the delivery of services. It provides inspiration and motivation for workers to perform.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Qualities of Good Leadership</p>
<p>These are numerous, as the following list suggests:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p><strong>Vision</strong>: Good leaders have a vision of the future and lead their people to their vision. A vision must be shared or it will be useless.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Creativity</strong>: A good leader is creative – able to come up with new ideas and ways of doing things.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Awareness</strong>: Good leaders have the ability to perceive opportunities in the environment within which they are operating, to enable them to utilize the context to achieve the intended objectives.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Initiative:</strong> This is exercised whenever effort is concentrated on a specific activity, to start something that was not going on before, to stop something that was occurring, or to shift the direction and character of the effort.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Inquisitive:</strong> This is the ability to gain access to facts and data from people or other information sources. The quality of information may depend on a leader's thoroughness, keenness and commitment. A leader who is keen to learn as much as possible about work activities is more likely to gain quality information than one who ignores the need for enquiry.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Supportive:</strong> A good leader is supportive to the led. The leader in return also gets support so that the vision and mission are realized.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Advocacy:</strong> This means taking a position in support of a cause, e.g., creating awareness on cost sharing. A leader has the ability to convince or persuade, and is prepared to take a stand.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Making decisions:</strong> This involves choosing or selecting from two or more courses of action. It may involve choosing an intervention or how best available resources can be effectively used.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Critiquing:</strong> Good leaders are able to give (and accept) constructive critique and feedback. Management teams need to use the "Critique Approach" when conducting supervision, counselling and guidance of their subordinates.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="leadership-styles">Leadership Styles<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#leadership-styles" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It is generally accepted that leadership style – that is, the way leadership ability is exercised – can be grouped into five³ categories:</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Autocratic/dictatorship:</strong> Autocratic leaders use their legitimate authority to suppress their followers in order to realize their own goals.</li>
<li><strong>Democratic:</strong> Democratic leadership is about majority rule; the leader goes by the decision of the majority. The weakness of this type of leadership is that the minority are suppressed and their views are rarely heard.</li>
<li><strong>Laissez-faire:</strong> This is a leadership style in which the leader provides minimal direction and gives employees power to determine goals, make decisions and resolve problems on their own. This style can be effective when employees are highly skilled, experienced, self-</li>
</ul>
<p>motivated and well educated. Otherwise, it can result in chaos.</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Paternalistic</strong>: These leaders act as fathers to their followers.</li>
<li><strong>Charismatic leadership</strong>: The leader has a mag-netic personality that in itself attracts people and instils loyalty. This type of leader is normally referred to as a born leader.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="leadership-skills">Leadership Skills<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#leadership-skills" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>As might be surmised from the previous section, leadership involves the following important skills:</p>
<ul>
<li>Getting and giving information</li>
<li>Understanding group needs and characteristics</li>
<li>Knowing and understanding group resources</li>
<li>Controlling the group</li>
<li>Counselling</li>
<li>Setting the example</li>
<li>Representing the group</li>
<li>Planning</li>
<li>Evaluating – progress, performance, people</li>
<li>Sharing leadership</li>
<li>Managing learning</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-difference-between-managers-and-leaders">The Difference between Managers and Leaders<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-difference-between-managers-and-leaders" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It is important to understand the difference between management and leadership. While management deals with the administrative aspects of a manager's work (planning, organizing, monitoring), leadership deals with the interpersonal aspects (inspiration, motivation, influence).</p>
<p>Qualities of both a manager and a leader are necessary. While performing managerial or administrative roles (planning, organizing and controlling), managers are often called upon to take up leadership roles that deal with the interpersonal aspects of their job (inspiration, motivation, influence) that may have a long-lasting impact on individuals and the group.</p>
<p>A manager without leadership skills is an admini-strator, while a creative, far-sighted leader who is un-able to implement a vision (without managerial skills) will confuse and lose the</p>
<p>team. There may be only one or two designated leaders in an organization, such as the director or team leader, but leadership qualities can be found in many others within the team.</p>
<p>Clearly, the managers must perform both leadership and management functions. The major responsibilities of a manager are to operate and maintain the organization efficiently, ensuring that it provides useful services to clients and the community. Managers tend to be problem-solvers, seeking better ways to use their resources to get the job done. Effective managers bring order and consistency to programmes.</p>
<p>Leaders, on the other hand, are pathfinders; they branch out more in their thinking. They are concerned with building the organization for the future, providing direction, securing new resources, developing new capacities, preparing the organization to meet challenges and take advantage of emerging opportunities, adapting to change, and networking.</p>
<p>In summary:</p>
<ul>
<li>Management is a science; leadership is an art.</li>
<li>Managers manage things; leaders lead people.</li>
<li>Managers run institutions; leaders build institutions.</li>
<li>Managers are busy; leaders make time.</li>
</ul>
<p>We can further summarize leadership as indicated below:</p>
<ol>
<li>Management is about path following; leadership is path finding</li>
<li>Management is about doing things right; leadership is about doing the right things</li>
<li>Management is about planning and budgeting; leadership is about establishing direction</li>
<li>Management is about controlling and problem solving; leadership is about motivating and inspiring</li>
</ol>
<p>Difference Between Managers and Leaders</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Manager</th><th>Leader</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Administers</td><td>Innovates</td></tr><tr><td>Is a ‘copy’</td><td>Is an ‘original’</td></tr><tr><td>Maintains</td><td>Develops</td></tr><tr><td>Focuses on systems and structures</td><td>Focuses on people</td></tr></tbody></table>
<table><thead><tr><th>Focuses on control</th><th>Inspires trust</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Takes a short-range view</td><td>Has a long-range perspective</td></tr><tr><td>Asks how and when</td><td>Asks what and why</td></tr><tr><td>Imitates</td><td>Originates</td></tr><tr><td>Accepts the status quo</td><td>Challenges the status quo</td></tr><tr><td>Is a classic ‘good soldier’</td><td>Is his or her own person</td></tr><tr><td>Does things right</td><td>Does the right thing</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="theories-of-leadership">Theories of leadership<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#theories-of-leadership" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Theories of leadership Interest in leadership increased during the early part of the twentieth century. Early leadership theories focused on what qualities distinguished between leaders and followers, while subsequent theories looked at other variables such as situational factors and skill levels. While many different leadership theories have emerged, most can be classified as one of eight major types:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="1-great-man-theories">1. Great Man Theories:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-great-man-theories" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Great man theories assume that the capacity for leadership is inherent – that great leaders are born, not made. These theories often portray great leaders as heroic, mythic and destined to rise to leadership when needed. The term "Great Man" was used because, at the time, leadership was thought of primarily as a male quality, especially in terms of military leadership.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="2-trait-theories">2. Trait Theories:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-trait-theories" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Similar in some ways to "Great Man" theories, trait theories assume that people inherit certain qualities and traits that make them better suited to leadership. Trait theories often identify particular personality or behavioral characteristics shared by leaders. If particular traits are key features of leadership, then how do we explain people who possess those qualities but are not leaders? This question is one of the difficulties in using trait theories to explain leadership.</p>
<ol start="3">
<li>Contingency Theories:</li>
</ol>
<p>Contingency theories of leadership focus on particular variables related to the environment that might determine which particular style of leadership is best suited for the situation. According to this theory, no leadership style is best in all situations. Success depends upon a number of variables, including the leadership style, qualities of the followers and aspects of the situation.</p>
<ol start="4">
<li>Situational Theories:</li>
</ol>
<p>Situational theories propose that leaders choose the best course of action based upon situational variables. Different styles of leadership may be more appropriate for certain types of decision-making. For example, in a situation where the leader is the most knowledgeable and experienced member of a group, an authoritarian style might be most appropriate. In other instances where group members are skilled experts, a democratic style would be more effective.</p>
<ol start="5">
<li>Behavioral Theories:</li>
</ol>
<p>Behavioral theories of leadership are based upon the belief that great leaders are made, not born. Rooted in behaviorism, this leadership theory focuses on the actions of leaders not on mental qualities or internal states. According to this theory, people can learn to become leaders through teaching and observation.</p>
<ol start="6">
<li>Participative Theories:</li>
</ol>
<p>Participative leadership theories suggest that the ideal leadership style is one that takes the input of others into account. These leaders encourage participation and contributions from group members and help group members feel more relevant and committed to the decision-making process. In participative theories, however, the leader retains the right to allow the input of others.</p>
<ol start="7">
<li>Management Theories:</li>
</ol>
<p>Management theories, also known as transactional theories, focus on the role of supervision, organization and group performance. These theories base leadership on a system of rewards</p>
<p>and punishments. Managerial theories are often used in business; when employees are successful, they are rewarded; when they fail, they are reprimanded or punished.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="8-relationship-theories">8. Relationship Theories:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#8-relationship-theories" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Relationship theories, also known as transformational theories, focus upon the connections formed between leaders and followers. Transformational leaders motivate and inspire people by helping group members see the importance and higher good of the task. These leaders are focused on the performance of group members, but also want each person to fulfill his or her potential. Leaders with this style often have high ethical and moral standards.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="change-management">Change Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#change-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="managing-change">Managing Change<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#managing-change" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Organizations are designed to fit the environment in which they operate at a given time. Their environments are constantly changing in varying degrees. Change is therefore inevitable in any organization that seeks to maintain its relevance. Managers deal with many organized groups representing various interests. Most of these groups exert power over operations of organizations. While they may be able to do little or nothing to change these forces, it is nevertheless up to the manager to anticipate and integrate these various forces that are bound to affect his operations.</p>
<p>Different types of change require different strategies to implement the change successfully. Managers must anticipate changes in the environment that will require future adjustments in the organizations designs. Change management is a systematic approach to dealing with change, both from the perspective of an organization and at the level of the individual.</p>
<p>Change management is a systematic approach to dealing with change, both from the perspective of an organization and on the individual level. Change management means to plan, initiate, realize, control and finally stabilize change processes, whether corporate or personal. Change may cover such diverse problems as, for example, strategic direction or personal development programmes for staff.</p>
<p>1</p>
<p>The Importance of Change Management</p>
<p>The capacity to manage change is the most important characteristic of leaders who succeed. This capacity depends on seven essential abilities:</p>
<ul>
<li>To be capable of adapting to the environment and be conscious of the gap between what is done and what can be done; to appreciate but never be satisfied with the situation as it is today and pay more attention to what does not function than to what functions.</li>
<li>To be able to question the present reality.</li>
<li>To be able to communicate a compelling vision of the direction the organization must take.</li>
<li>To have the capacity to create a coalition of followers and supporters.</li>
<li>To be capable of building a team capable of making change happen.</li>
<li>To persist and persevere.</li>
<li>To share with others the credit and recognition brought by success.</li>
</ul>
<p>Types of Change</p>
<p>Change is the continuous adaptation of corporate strategies and structures to changing external conditions. It may take several forms, as described below:</p>
<p><strong>Developmental Change</strong> Developmental change occurs when an organization makes an improvement to its current way of doing business. If an organization decided to improve its processes, methods or performance standards, this would be considered developmental change.</p>
<p><strong>Transformational Change</strong> Transformational change occurs after the transition period. Transformational change may involve both developmental and transitional change, and it is common for the two types to occur in tandem. This happens mainly when organizations are faced with the emergence of radically different technologies, significant changes in supply and demand, unexpected competition, lack of revenue, or other major shifts in how they do business. Formal changes are the responses of an organization to major internal forces and external pressures (change drivers).</p>
<p><strong>Transitional Change</strong> Transitional change is more intrusive than developmental change, as it</p>
<p>replaces existing processes or procedures with something that is completely new to the organization. The period when the old process is being dismantled and the new process is being implemented is called the transitional phase.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="guidelines-for-change-management">Guidelines for Change Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#guidelines-for-change-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Resistance to change can be avoided or reduced by doing the following:</p>
<p>1.) Planning change carefully:</p>
<ul>
<li>Identify problems and their underlying causes.</li>
<li>Formulate alternative actions to solve the problems.
Select the most feasible action(s).</li>
<li>Establish an implementation plan.</li>
</ul>
<p>2.) Involving those who will be affected by change right from the planning stage:</p>
<ul>
<li>Explain the benefits, for both the organization and the people, and also indicate the disadvantages that are likely to arise.</li>
<li>Introduce change gradually.</li>
<li>Be patient and tolerant during change; give support and help.</li>
<li>Provide training that may be needed.</li>
<li>Monitor change.</li>
<li>Communicate frequently with people during and after change in order to identify snags and problems.</li>
<li>Be firm about the end results but flexible about getting there.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="kurt-lewin-s-change-model">Kurt Lewin's Change Model<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#kurt-lewin-s-change-model" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>An early model of change developed by Kurt Lewin (1890–1947), one of the modern pioneers of social, organizational and applied psychology, described change as a three-stage process:</p>
<p>1.) "Unfreezing": It involves overcoming inertia and dismantling the existing "mind set". Defence mechanisms have to be bypassed.</p>
<p>2.) "Change": This is typically a period of confusion and transition. We are aware that the old ways are being challenged, but we do not yet have a clear picture of what we are replacing them with.</p>
<p>3.) "Refreezing": The new mindset is crystallizing and one's comfort level is returning to</p>
<p>previous levels. This is often misquoted as “refreezing” (Lewin, 1947).</p>
<p><strong>Note:</strong> <em>Change is inevitable in the current complex environment. It has been said that change is the only constant. A leader would learn change practices that produce results.</em></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="resistance-to-change">Resistance to change<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#resistance-to-change" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Change is actually a frightening word for many people. People resist and try to avoid changes that will leave them worse off than they are now. But, resistance may be met even if the proposed change is neutral, or beneficial to the persons concerned.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="why-people-resist-change">Why people resist change<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#why-people-resist-change" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>They believe that is not <em>in their interest</em> to change</li>
<li>They think the change is the <em>wrong thing</em> to do, if you knew what I knew</li>
<li>They are <em>skeptical</em> about the possibility of success</li>
<li>They <em>don’t understand why</em> change is happening</li>
<li>It isn’t relevant to them</li>
<li>They don’t understand exactly <em>what change</em> is required</li>
<li>They don’t have the <em>time or resources</em> to change</li>
<li>They don’t think that they will be able to change (<em>Fear of failure</em>)</li>
<li>They feel they were not involved in the decision to change</li>
<li>Lack of conviction that change is needed</li>
<li>Dislike of imposed change</li>
<li>Dislike of surprises</li>
<li>Fear of the unknown</li>
<li>Reluctance to deal with unpopular issues fear of</li>
<li>inadequacy and failure</li>
<li>Disturbed practices, habits and relations</li>
<li>Lack of respect and trust in the person promoting change</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="strategies-for-dealing-with-change-resistance">Strategies for dealing with change resistance<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#strategies-for-dealing-with-change-resistance" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li><strong>Education &amp; Communication:</strong></li>
</ol>
<p>One of the best ways to overcome resistance to change is to educate people about the change effort beforehand. Up-front communication and education helps employees see the logic in the change effort. This reduces unfounded and incorrect rumors concerning the effects of change in the organization</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-participation-involvement">2. Participation &amp; Involvement:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-participation-involvement" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>When employees are involved in the change effort they are more likely to buy into change rather than resist it. This approach is likely to lower resistance more so than merely hoping people will acquiesce to change</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-facilitation-support">3. Facilitation &amp; Support:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-facilitation-support" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Managers can head-off potential resistance by being supportive of employees during difficult times. Managerial support helps employees deal with fear and anxiety during a transition period. This approach is concerned with provision of special training, counseling, time off work.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-negotiation-and-agreement">4. Negotiation and Agreement:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-negotiation-and-agreement" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Managers can combat resistance by offering incentives to employees not to resist change. This can be done by allowing change resistors to veto elements of change that are threatening, or change resistors can be offered incentives to go elsewhere in the company in order to avoid having to experience the change effort.
